Attribution by Cause (Sabab)
Maturidi describes the first mode of attribution: by cause (sabab).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first way of attributing actions to God: by cause (sabab), where God is the ultimate cause even if the immediate act is from humans.

Core Idea
God's actions are attributed by cause, not direct performance.

Maturidi begins by explaining that beyond direct creation, actions are attributed to God in two ways. The first is by cause: God commands good and leaves evil, so He is the ultimate source. This lesson unpacks that concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first way
Read the Arabic passage describing the first mode of attribution and its translation. Notice the phrase 'one is by cause'.
2
Understand 'by cause' meaning
Maturidi says God commands good and leaves evil. This causal role means He is the origin, even if humans perform the act.
The phrase 'even if the reality of the act is not his' is key.
3
Reflect on human responsibility
Ask yourself: how does this preserve human agency while affirming God's ultimate causality?
This connects to broader kalam debates on free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا في الإضافة إلى الله : إنها تخرج على وجهين سوى حقيقة الفعل : أحدهما بالسبب الأفعال الأمر بالخيرات والتخلية فى الشرور ، وقد تضاف مع رجوع قدرة الذي كان منهم الأفعال إلى من له الأسباب وإن لم يكن حقيقتها له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والثانى أن الإضافة إليه عند المحنة بما له بها حال التصديق والتكذيب كما أضيف إلى القرآن زادهم إيمانا ورجسا ، وإلى الدعاء أنه زادهم نفورا ، والى القوم أن أنسوهم ذكر الله وإلى الأصنام أن أهلكن كثيرا من الناس بما عبدوا ، كانت أفعال البشر أولئك ، فمثله الإضافة إلى الله. وقد يحتمل الأحوال كما أضيف إلى الدنيا الغرور وإلى زينتها بما هي تظهر ما يكون مثله الغرور ، وإن لم يكن منها حق الفعل، وكذا ما أضيف إلى القرى الخاوية على عروشها والقيود من النطق، وإلى البهائم من الشكاية مما لو كانت تنطق بقول ، فمثله في الإضافة إلى الله . بما منه من الإمهال وإظهار النعم الذى كاد أن يكون حجة لهم في الرضا بأفعالهم ؛ ولذلك ظنوا أن الله أمرهم بما هم فيه من الأفعال بالإمهال والتأخير ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And they said regarding attribution to God: That it occurs in two ways besides the reality of the act: One is by cause: the acts are commanding good and leaving alone in evils. And it may be attributed with the return of the power of those who performed the acts to the one who has the causes, even if the reality of the act is not his. And there is no power except with Allah. The second is that attribution to Him in trial is by the state that He has in relation to belief and disbelief, just as it is attributed to the Quran that it increased them in faith and abomination, and to the supplication that it increased them in aversion, and to the people that they made them forget the remembrance of God, and to the idols that they destroyed many of the people because of what they worshipped. Those were acts of humans. So similarly is attribution to God. And it may involve states, just as it is attributed to this world that it deludes, and to its adornment because it shows what is like delusion, even if the real act is not from it. Likewise what is attributed to the villages collapsed on their roofs and to restraints from speech, and to animals from complaint, as if they were speaking in expression. So similarly in attribution to God, due to what is from Him of respite and manifestation of blessings that almost became a proof for them in being pleased with their actions. Therefore they thought that God commanded them to do the actions they are in, by respite and delay. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage details two ways of attributing actions to God besides direct creation (ḥaqīqat al-fiʻl). The first is by cause (sabab): God commands good and leaves alone evil, so actions are attributed to God as the ultimate cause even though the immediate power is from the human agent. The second is by state (ḥāl): in trials, God is described as having a state that influences belief or disbelief, similar to how the Quran is said to increase faith for some and abomination for others, or how supplication increases aversion. Analogies include worldly delusion, ruined villages, animals complaining—all attributions that are not literal creation but based on context. Maturidi notes that some mistakenly think God's respite and blessings imply divine approval of their actions, leading them to believe God commanded their deeds through delay.

Key Terms
إضافة (attribution)
سبب (cause)
حقيقة الفعل (reality of the act)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attribution by cause means God directly performs the act.
Correction: It means God is the ultimate cause, not the direct agent; the immediate act is from the human.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, the first way of attributing actions to God is:
A) By creation
B) By cause
C) By state
D) By acquisition

Key Takeaway
The first mode of attribution is by cause: God commands good and leaves evil, so He is the ultimate source even though the act's reality is from the human.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you truly feel that God is the ultimate cause, or do you unconsciously credit your own power? What would change in your gratitude if you constantly remembered that the act's reality is yours but the causation is from Him?
?
If you commit a sin, do you blame yourself entirely, or do you recognize that God left you to your own choice? How does this understanding affect the sincerity of your repentance and your reliance on His mercy?
?
The passage ends with 'there is no power except with Allah.' In what area of your life are you still acting as if your own power is sufficient? What step can you take today to consciously return that power to its true Source?

Attribution by State (Hal) and Warning
Maturidi details the second mode: by state (hal) and the resulting misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second way of attributing actions to God: by state (hal), and recognize the mistake of thinking God's respite implies command of all actions.

Core Idea
Attribution by state links God to actions through His state in trials, not direct creation.

Maturidi now explains the second mode: attribution by state. This involves God's state in relation to belief and disbelief, using analogies from the Quran and worldly examples. He also warns against misinterpreting God's respite as approval of all actions.

1
Read the passage on the second way
Read the Arabic passage describing attribution by state and its translation. Note the examples given: Quran, supplication, people, idols.
2
Understand the analogies
Maturidi compares this attribution to worldly examples: world's delusion, ruined villages, animals' complaint. These are not literal creation but based on state.
The key is that the 'real act' is not from the object.
3
Identify the warning
The passage ends with a warning: some mistakenly think God's respite (imhal) and blessings imply He commands their actions. Understand why this is wrong.
This connects to the broader issue of qadar.

Justice as the Middle Path: Refuting Extremes
Maturidi defines justice and refutes two extreme sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of justice ('adl) as the affirmation that both God and the servant are involved in actions, and how this refutes the extremes of Murji'ah and Qadariyyah.

Core Idea
Justice is the balanced middle between denying human agency and denying divine action.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi introduces the concept of justice ('adl) by critiquing two deviant groups: the Murji'ah, who remove human responsibility, and the Qadariyyah, who remove divine management. He then defines justice as holding both truths together: God is the Creator of all, yet He is not unjust to His servants. This foundational idea sets the stage for the Maturidi doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extreme groups
Read the first few lines to understand what the Murji'ah and the Qadariyyah claim about human actions. Note how each group errs by neglecting either divine or human agency.
Murji'ah defer actions to God; Qadariyyah attribute them to God but deny His management.
2
Grasp the definition of justice
See how Maturidi defines justice as 'affirming both matters'—that actions are from God and from the servant. He supports this with Quranic verses showing God as Creator and as not unjust.
The verses emphasize both divine power and human responsibility.
3
Reflect on the necessity of this balance
Consider why Maturidi calls this justice. Think about how denying either side leads to theological error.
This balance preserves both God's sovereignty and human accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما روى من لعن المرجئة والقدرية . إن المرجئة أرجأت الأفعال إلى الله ولم تجعلها للعبد ، والقدرية أثبتتها لله على ما تنسب الخلق إلى الله تعالى ، ولم تجعل الله فيها تدبيرا . والعدل هو القول بتحقيق الأمرين ؛ ليكون الله موصوفا بما وصف به نفسه محمودا به كما قال : " خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ " وقال : " فَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ " ، وليكون عدلا مفصلا كما قال : " وَمَا رَبُّكَ لظَلَّامٍ لِلعَبِيدِ " ، وقال : " وَلَوْلَا فَضْلُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْكُمْ وَرَحْمَتُهُ لاتَّبَعْتُم الشَّيْطَانَ إِلَّا قَلِيلا " ، ثم الدليل على لزوم القول بهذا - مع ما فيما بينا كفابة وجود أحوال فى أفعال العبد لا يبلغها أوهامهم ولا يقدرها عقولهم ، وأحوال فيها ينتهى إليها قصدهم وتبلغها عقولهم ، فثبت أنها من الوجه الأول ليست لهم ، ومن الوجه الثانى لهم . فالأول كتصوير خروج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود وكأخذ الفعل من قدر الجو والمكان والحد الذي لو أحب أن يعود إليه ما أمكنه بلا فيه ، والثانى نحو التحرك والسكون بالمنهى والمأمور به ، ثبت من الوجه الأول ليس لهم ، ومن الثانى لهم . ولو جاز تحقيق فعلهم من الوجه أن فعلهم من الأول على ظهور خروجه من قصدهم وجملتهم مختلفة مما ذكر ، وعجزهم عن العود إلى مثله الجاز كون العالم على ما عليه بمن لا يقدر ولا يعلم ولا يعرف مقادير كل شيء ، ويجوز أيضا آيات على ما هي عليه بالبشر وإن لم يكن بمثلها علم ولا عليها قدرة . فإذ ازمهم القول بالصانع والرسل بخروج الذي ذكرت عن وسع الخلق فمثله أفعال الخلق ؛ ولذلك قال الله سبحانه : " لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ " وأوجب أن تشابه الخلق من الوجه الذي قلت تماثلا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
What has been narrated concerning the curse of the Murji'ah and the Qadariyyah. Indeed, the Murji'ah deferred actions to God and did not attribute them to the servant, and the Qadariyyah attributed them to God in the sense of attributing creation to God, and did not make God have any management in them. Justice is the statement affirming both matters; so that God may be described with what He described Himself, praised thereby, as He said: 'Creator of everything' and He said: 'He is over all things competent,' and so that He may be just and detailed, as He said: 'And your Lord is not unjust to His servants,' and He said: 'And if it were not for the favor of God upon you and His mercy, you would have followed Satan, except a few.' Then the proof for the necessity of affirming this—along with what we have stated suffices—is the existence of states in the actions of the servant that their imaginations do not reach nor their intellects measure, and states in which their intention terminates and their intellects attain. So it is established that from the first aspect they are not theirs, and from the second aspect they are theirs. The first is like depicting the emergence of a thing from non-existence to existence, and like taking the action from the capacity of the air, place, and limit such that if one wished to return to it, he could not do so without it; the second is like movement and stillness in the prohibited and commanded. It is established that from the first aspect it is not theirs, and from the second it is theirs. And if it were permissible to affirm their action from the aspect that their action from the first—given its apparent emergence from their intention and their entirety differing from what was mentioned, and their inability to return to its like—then it would be permissible for the world to be as it is from one who does not have power, knowledge, or knowledge of the measures of everything, and also permissible for signs to be as they are by humans even if they have no knowledge of their like nor power over them. So since they are compelled to affirm the Maker and the Messengers due to what I mentioned being beyond the capacity of creatures, likewise the actions of creatures; therefore God Almighty said: 'Nothing is like Him,' and He necessitated that creatures resemble [Him] from the aspect I mentioned as likeness, and there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage defines justice ('adl) in the context of divine actions and human acquisition, and provides the first proof for affirming that actions belong to both God and the servant in different respects. Maturidi begins by refuting two extreme groups: the Murji'ah, who attribute actions entirely to God and deny human agency, and the Qadariyyah, who attribute actions entirely to God in a way that denies His management. Justice, he states, is the affirmation of both aspects: God is the Creator of all things, yet He is not unjust to His servants, and His grace is necessary. Then he presents the proof: there are aspects of actions that human intellect cannot fathom—like bringing something from non-existence to existence—and aspects that are within human intention and capacity, like moving or sitting in response to commands. From the first aspect, actions are not the servant's; from the second, they are. If one denied this, it would lead to absurdities such as allowing the world to exist without a wise Creator, or allowing miracles to be performed by humans without divine power. Therefore, just as the inability to create compels belief in a Creator, the same logic applies to human actions: the creative aspect belongs to God alone, while the acquired aspect belongs to the servant, without resemblance (tamathul) in essence.

Key Terms
عدل
مرجئة
قدرية
خلق
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming human agency necessarily diminishes God's power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that justice requires both: God's creation does not contradict human acquisition.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi define 'justice' ('adl) in this passage?
A) Affirming that God creates all actions without any human role
B) Affirming that both God's creation and human acquisition are true
C) Affirming that humans create their own actions independently
D) Denying any role of God in human actions

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's justice is the middle path: God creates all actions, yet the servant genuinely acquires them, refuting both Murji'ah and Qadariyyah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when you succeed, do you attribute it entirely to God's creation and favor, or do you feel a sense of personal pride that edges out gratitude? When you fail, do you blame God's decree or take responsibility? Maturidi says both matter. Which extreme do you lean toward, and what one concrete step can you take today to restore the balance?
?
You've just read that justice is affirming both matters: God creates all and you are responsible. If you truly lived this, how would it change the way you prepare for a difficult task tomorrow? Would you rely more on God's help or on your own planning? What does your answer reveal about your current state of faith?
?
The Murji'ah and Qadariyyah are not just ancient sects; the same tendencies exist in your heart. When you commit a sin, do you feel it was 'ordained' and therefore not fully your fault (Murji'ah-like), or do you feel God had nothing to do with it (Qadariyyah-like)? Which tendency is more harmful to your repentance? What will you do about it tonight?

The Proof of the Two Aspects of Actions
Maturidi provides a rational proof for the two aspects of actions and a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that human actions have two aspects: one in which they are from God alone (creation) and one in which they are from the servant (acquisition), and the absurd consequences of denying this.

Core Idea
Human actions have two distinct aspects: one only God can create, the other the servant acquires.

After defining justice, Maturidi provides a rational proof to demonstrate that actions must be attributed to both God and the servant in different respects. He points to the obvious fact that some aspects of actions—like bringing something from non-existence to existence—are beyond human power, while other aspects—like moving or sitting—fall within human intention and capacity. Denying this distinction leads to absurdities, such as allowing that the world could be created by an ignorant being or that miracles could be performed by humans without divine power. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two states of actions
Read the part where Maturidi distinguishes between states that human imagination cannot reach (like creation ex nihilo) and states that human intention and intellect comprehend (like voluntary movement).
The first aspect is divine, the second is human.
2
Examine the examples given
Look at the examples: the first aspect includes the emergence from non-existence and the taking of action from the capacity of air, place, etc.; the second aspect includes movement and stillness in response to commands.
These clarify the two aspects.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Maturidi argues that if you deny that the first aspect is beyond human power, then it would be possible for an ignorant being to create the world or for humans to perform miracles. Since we affirm a wise Creator and prophets with miracles, we must accept the two aspects.
This proves that the creative aspect belongs to God alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما روى من لعن المرجئة والقدرية . إن المرجئة أرجأت الأفعال إلى الله ولم تجعلها للعبد ، والقدرية أثبتتها لله على ما تنسب الخلق إلى الله تعالى ، ولم تجعل الله فيها تدبيرا . والعدل هو القول بتحقيق الأمرين ؛ ليكون الله موصوفا بما وصف به نفسه محمودا به كما قال : " خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ " وقال : " فَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ " ، وليكون عدلا مفصلا كما قال : " وَمَا رَبُّكَ لظَلَّامٍ لِلعَبِيدِ " ، وقال : " وَلَوْلَا فَضْلُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْكُمْ وَرَحْمَتُهُ لاتَّبَعْتُم الشَّيْطَانَ إِلَّا قَلِيلا " ، ثم الدليل على لزوم القول بهذا - مع ما فيما بينا كفابة وجود أحوال فى أفعال العبد لا يبلغها أوهامهم ولا يقدرها عقولهم ، وأحوال فيها ينتهى إليها قصدهم وتبلغها عقولهم ، فثبت أنها من الوجه الأول ليست لهم ، ومن الوجه الثانى لهم . فالأول كتصوير خروج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود وكأخذ الفعل من قدر الجو والمكان والحد الذي لو أحب أن يعود إليه ما أمكنه بلا فيه ، والثانى نحو التحرك والسكون بالمنهى والمأمور به ، ثبت من الوجه الأول ليس لهم ، ومن الثانى لهم . ولو جاز تحقيق فعلهم من الوجه أن فعلهم من الأول على ظهور خروجه من قصدهم وجملتهم مختلفة مما ذكر ، وعجزهم عن العود إلى مثله الجاز كون العالم على ما عليه بمن لا يقدر ولا يعلم ولا يعرف مقادير كل شيء ، ويجوز أيضا آيات على ما هي عليه بالبشر وإن لم يكن بمثلها علم ولا عليها قدرة . فإذ ازمهم القول بالصانع والرسل بخروج الذي ذكرت عن وسع الخلق فمثله أفعال الخلق ؛ ولذلك قال الله سبحانه : " لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ " وأوجب أن تشابه الخلق من الوجه الذي قلت تماثلا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
What has been narrated concerning the curse of the Murji'ah and the Qadariyyah. Indeed, the Murji'ah deferred actions to God and did not attribute them to the servant, and the Qadariyyah attributed them to God in the sense of attributing creation to God, and did not make God have any management in them. Justice is the statement affirming both matters; so that God may be described with what He described Himself, praised thereby, as He said: 'Creator of everything' and He said: 'He is over all things competent,' and so that He may be just and detailed, as He said: 'And your Lord is not unjust to His servants,' and He said: 'And if it were not for the favor of God upon you and His mercy, you would have followed Satan, except a few.' Then the proof for the necessity of affirming this—along with what we have stated suffices—is the existence of states in the actions of the servant that their imaginations do not reach nor their intellects measure, and states in which their intention terminates and their intellects attain. So it is established that from the first aspect they are not theirs, and from the second aspect they are theirs. The first is like depicting the emergence of a thing from non-existence to existence, and like taking the action from the capacity of the air, place, and limit such that if one wished to return to it, he could not do so without it; the second is like movement and stillness in the prohibited and commanded. It is established that from the first aspect it is not theirs, and from the second it is theirs. And if it were permissible to affirm their action from the aspect that their action from the first—given its apparent emergence from their intention and their entirety differing from what was mentioned, and their inability to return to its like—then it would be permissible for the world to be as it is from one who does not have power, knowledge, or knowledge of the measures of everything, and also permissible for signs to be as they are by humans even if they have no knowledge of their like nor power over them. So since they are compelled to affirm the Maker and the Messengers due to what I mentioned being beyond the capacity of creatures, likewise the actions of creatures; therefore God Almighty said: 'Nothing is like Him,' and He necessitated that creatures resemble [Him] from the aspect I mentioned as likeness, and there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage defines justice ('adl) in the context of divine actions and human acquisition, and provides the first proof for affirming that actions belong to both God and the servant in different respects. Maturidi begins by refuting two extreme groups: the Murji'ah, who attribute actions entirely to God and deny human agency, and the Qadariyyah, who attribute actions entirely to God in a way that denies His management. Justice, he states, is the affirmation of both aspects: God is the Creator of all things, yet He is not unjust to His servants, and His grace is necessary. Then he presents the proof: there are aspects of actions that human intellect cannot fathom—like bringing something from non-existence to existence—and aspects that are within human intention and capacity, like moving or sitting in response to commands. From the first aspect, actions are not the servant's; from the second, they are. If one denied this, it would lead to absurdities such as allowing the world to exist without a wise Creator, or allowing miracles to be performed by humans without divine power. Therefore, just as the inability to create compels belief in a Creator, the same logic applies to human actions: the creative aspect belongs to God alone, while the acquired aspect belongs to the servant, without resemblance (tamathul) in essence.

Key Terms

مرجئة
قدرية
خلق
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if humans are responsible for actions, then they must be the creators of those actions.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that humans are not creators; they acquire actions that God creates in them.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of human actions according to Maturidi's proof?
A) The voluntary movement in response to commands
B) The aspect that is beyond human power, like creation from non-existence
C) The intention and choice of the servant
D) The outcome of the action

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that actions have a divine aspect (creation) and a human aspect (acquisition); denying this leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a good deed, do you thank God for creating the action or credit yourself entirely? Which aspect of the action do you tend to forget, and how does Maturidi's proof challenge your assumption of self-sufficiency?
?
The passage says the first aspect (existence of the action) is beyond your capacity. Have you ever felt that you created something entirely by yourself, like a skill or achievement? How would it change your humility to truly believe that every action has a divine side you cannot replicate?
?
If you deny the two aspects, the world could be from an ignorant being. Do you ever act as if your actions are independent of God's will? What would it mean for your daily life—your work, worship, or relationships—to genuinely believe that every moment has a creative act of God you cannot produce on your own?

The Imperfection Implied by Human Power Being Given and Removed
Maturidi argues that if human power is given then removed, God's power would be subject to change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that human power cannot be given and then removed because it would imply change in God's power.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot change; therefore human power is not independently acquired.

Maturidi addresses a subtle theological problem: if God gives power to a human and then removes it, does that imply a change in God? He argues that such a view leads to the absurd conclusion that the servant becomes like a lord. He also explores the ambiguity of human acts, showing that attributing acts to other than God leads to confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a condensed style here.
2
Identify the main argument
The passage presents a reductio ad absurdum: if power is given and then removed, it implies change in God, which is impossible. Find the key sentence where this conclusion is drawn.
3
Understand the ambiguity of acts
Maturidi discusses movement and stillness as indistinguishable in appearance. Why does he bring this up? How does it connect to the main argument about power?
This section reinforces that acts cannot be attributed to multiple agents without causing theological ambiguity.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this argument safeguards God's perfection. If God's power were subject to change, He would not be the Necessary Being.
This connects to the broader doctrine of divine immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أقدر العبد على ذلك ذهبت عنه القدرة فإذا قدرته زالت عنه ، وصار قادرا بقدرة تزول ، ومن ذلك وصفه فهو عبد لا رب ، والله الموفق . مع ما كانت الحركة والسكون ليسا بمخالفين في رأي العين لما كانا عليه ، ولا سبيل للناظر إلى التفريق بينهما ، ولولا حقيقة الاشتباه لاحتمل التفريق ، وفى تشابه الفعل لزوم القول فيها بما له وجبت التسمية فى أحدهما ، وفى ذلك تشابه ؛ لأن استواء الأفعال في الشاهد يوجب تشابه الفاعلين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
When He enables the servant for that, the power leaves him; so when He enables, the power departs from him, and he becomes capable with a power that departs. And from that His description is that He is a servant, not a Lord, and Allah is the Granter of success. For movement and stillness are not different in the eye's view from what they were, and the observer has no way to distinguish between them. Were it not for the reality of ambiguity, distinction would be possible. In the similarity of the act, there is necessity of the statement regarding them according to what necessitates naming in one of them, and in that is ambiguity; because the equivalence of acts in the witnessed world necessitates the equivalence of the agents. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 830

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the idea that God gives power to a servant and then removes it, because that would imply change in God's power. Maturidi argues that if human power were given and then taken away, the servant would become capable with a power that is transitory, and that description would make the servant not a lord. He then discusses the ambiguity of acts: movement and stillness appear the same, yet we cannot distinguish them. The similarity of acts in the world implies similarity of agents, so attributing acts to other than God leads to theological ambiguity. The argument is that if human acts are not directly from God, then the agent's nature becomes ambiguous.

Correction: Maturidi shows that if power is given and then removed, it implies a change in God's power, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

How does this lesson challenge that false belief, and what verse or saying can you hold onto to remind you of God's unchanging nature?
?
Maturidi argues that the observer cannot distinguish between two similar acts. Think of a good deed you did recently—did you consider it a result of your own effort or a blessing from God? Write down one practical step you can take this week to actively attribute all your actions to God's giving, not your own independent power.

Deficient Power and Divine Perfection
Maturidi defines deficient power and argues it cannot apply to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of deficient power and the argument for why Allah's power must be absolutely unlimited.

Core Idea
God's power cannot be like that of any creature.

In this brief but essential passage, Imam Maturidi draws a clear line between the power of creatures and the power of the Creator. He defines what makes power deficient — its limitation to specific things — and then shows that if Allah's power were similarly limited, it would be unworthy of Him. This simple argument grounds the Islamic doctrine that Allah is omnipotent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the original Arabic text along with the literal English translation. Let the words of Maturidi settle before analyzing them.
Pay attention to the terms 'القدرة الناقصة' (deficient power) and 'صفة المخلوق' (attribute of the created).
2
Identify the definition of deficient power
Maturidi gives a precise definition: deficient power is the kind of power that belongs to every creature — they have power over some things but not over others.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the reasoning: if Allah lacked power over something His servant has, then Allah's power would be like that of a deficient creature. Since that is impossible, Allah must have power over everything.
Notice how Maturidi uses a conditional (if... then) to arrive at the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن القدرة الناقصة هى التى تكون لكل أحد من الخلق ، ولكل قدرة على ما ليس لغيره فإذا لم يكن الله قدرة على ما لعبده ، فإذا قدرته نحو قدرة كل منقوص ، جل الله عن صفة المخلوق ، وبالله التوفيق.
As for deficient power, it is that which belongs to every one of the creation, and every power is over what is not for another. So if Allah does not have power over what His servant has, then His power is like the power of every deficient one. Exalted is Allah above the attribute of the created, and success is from Allah.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This is a clear definition and argument. Maturidi defines deficient power (al-qudra al-naqisa) as the kind of power that creatures have: each creature has power over some things but not over others. If God were to lack power over something that His servant has, then God's power would be like the power of a deficient creature. This is impossible for Allah. So God must have power over everything.

Key Terms
القدرة الناقصة
صفة المخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah has power over all things, He must also have power to do evil or unjust actions.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is about the scope of power, not its moral content. Allah has power over all things, but He does not choose evil because His attributes are perfect and He is free from deficiency. The passage does not discuss moral actions, only logical possibility.

Checkpoint
What is deficient power according to Maturidi?
A) Power that is weak
B) Power that belongs to everyone but is limited to their own domain
C) Power that is given and then removed
D) Power that is only for God

Key Takeaway
Allah's power is absolute and unlimited, unlike the limited power of creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that feels impossible, do you truly believe that Allah's power extends over that specific thing, or do you unconsciously limit Him like a creature? What one step can you take today to act on His absolute power?
?
If Allah's power were deficient even in one area, your trust in Him would be shattered. Is there any part of your life where you hold back full reliance on Allah, as if He cannot handle it? How can you surrender that area to Him right now?
?
The passage declares Allah exalted above any likeness to creation. Examine your heart: do you ever imagine Allah's power as 'greater but similar' to human strength? Repent of that comparison and ask Him to deepen your awe of His unique, limitless power.

The Necessity of God's Absolute Power
Maturidi argues that if anything were outside God's power, trust in His promises would be undermined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that nothing can be outside God's power, and how this conclusion is necessary for maintaining trust in divine promises.

Core Idea
If God could be powerless over anything, His promises would be meaningless.

Imam al-Maturidi presents a concise argument for the absolute scope of divine power. He shows that any limitation would unravel the entire fabric of religious belief, because trust in God's promises and threats depends on His ability to fulfill them. This passage from Kitab al-Tawhid is a masterful use of reductio ad absurdum to establish a foundational truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi uses rhetorical questions to drive his point.
2
Identify the hypothetical premise
Maturidi starts by supposing something that is under power could be outside God's power. This is the premise he will refute.
The phrase 'لو جاز' indicates a hypothetical.
3
Trace the consequence for belief
He then asks how we could believe in God's promise and threat if He were unable to act. This shows the practical implication of the premise.
The questions 'كيف نؤمن' and 'كيف يطمئن' highlight the absurdity.
4
Examine the further proof from revelation
Maturidi cites God's statement about creating the like, and contrasts it with inability to do a small act. This internal contradiction seals the argument.
The example of a gnat emphasizes the absurdity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that the premise is impossible; God's power must be absolute. Consider why this matters for Islamic theology.
This argument is foundational for the doctrine of divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه لوجاز خروج شيء هو تحت القدرة عن أن يكون الله عليه قدرة ، بل ليس هو شيئا واحدا ، بل لعله أكثر من جميع الخلق ، كيف نؤمن بوعده ووعيده ، وكيف يطمئن السامع إلى ما وعده من البعث أن يكون ، وما أخبر أنه لو شاء لخلق مثل الذى خلق ، وهو لا يقدر على فعل بعوض ، فضلا عن فعل هو أقوى منه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also, if it were permissible for something that is under power to be such that Allah has no power over it — indeed it is not one thing, but perhaps it is more than all of creation — how then can we believe in His promise and His threat? And how can the listener be assured of what He promised of the resurrection? And He informed that if He willed, He would create the like of what He created, yet He is not able to do the act of a gnat, let alone an act that is stronger than it. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage argues reductio ad absurdum. If something could be outside God's power, then consider that that something might be very large, perhaps larger than all creation. Then God would not have power over it. But then we could not trust God's promises, because He might be unable to fulfill them. Also, God said He could create the like of what He created, but if He cannot even do a small act like a gnat's action, then He cannot do greater acts. This is impossible.

Key Terms
الوعيد
البعث
قدرة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can be limited without affecting the reliability of His promises.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if any limitation existed, trust in promises and threats would be impossible, because He might be unable to fulfill them.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Maturidi draw from the possibility of something being outside God's power?
A) God's wisdom would be questioned
B) Belief in His promises and threats would be impossible
C) The creation would be meaningless
D) Humans would become gods

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument proves that absolute divine power is necessary for religious trust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live with a deep, practical trust that God has absolute power over everything in your life—your health, finances, future? What is one specific fear that you have been acting as if God cannot handle? What would it look like to entrust it to Him today?
?
If you truly believed that God's threat of punishment is as sure as His promise of reward because of His absolute power, how would that change the way you treat a recurring sin you have normalized? What concrete step will you take this week to take His threat seriously?
?
The argument shows that any doubt in God's power makes faith unstable. Is there an area where you have been saying 'God can do anything' but secretly doubting He can change a difficult situation? Will you honestly confess that doubt to Him and ask for a heart that trusts His power without reservation?

God's Ownership Necessitates Creation
Maturidi argues that God's ownership necessitates creation of all things, including human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's absolute ownership of everything logically entails that He is the Creator of all things, including human actions.

Core Idea
God's ownership of all things necessarily means He created them.

This lesson presents a key argument from Imam Maturidi regarding divine ownership and creation. Maturidi establishes that since God is the Owner (Malik) of everything in the most absolute sense, His ownership demands that He be the Creator of all, including human acts. The passage carefully distinguishes God's ownership from created ownership to avoid a contradiction. Before diving into the text, consider what it means for something to be owned and how ownership relates to power and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text slowly, then its translation. Notice the repeated emphasis on 'ownership' (al-milk) and 'Creator' (khāliq).
This passage is dense; read twice.
2
Identify the logical argument
Maturidi uses a conditional: if God were not the owner/creator of human acts, then His ownership would be deficient. Trace how he proves that God must be the creator.
Pay attention to the contrast between God's ownership and created ownership.
3
Connect to the doctrine of qadar
This argument is part of the broader discussion on predestination (qadar). Think about how this view preserves God's sovereignty while acknowledging human agency.
The common misunderstanding is that this negates human responsibility; Maturidi affirms ultimate creation by God without denying human acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله إذ هو مالك كل شيء وملكه الأشياء ليس بما أوجب له فيه الملك لملك العبد ، بل هو بذاته مالك بما هو خالق كل شيء . فأما أن يكون غير مالك لفعل العباد ولا رب ها فيجب به أن يكون للعباد ذلك ، فيكون ربوبيته وملكه ملكا ناقصا ، وذلك لكل مخلوق يملك أشياء ، بل هو أكثر ; لأنه يملك وفعل غيره والله لا يملك ، وإذا ثبت له الملك فى كل شيء لزم القول بخلقه ; إذ لا يملكه العبد ، ويملك الأشياء بالقدرة عليها أو تمليك من له تلك ، فعله ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, since Allah is the Owner of everything, and His ownership of things is not because ownership for Him is necessitated by the ownership of the servant, but He is by His essence Owner because He is the Creator of everything. So if He is not the Owner of the actions of the servants nor their Lord, then it would be necessary that the servants have that, and then His lordship and ownership would be deficient ownership. And that is for every creature that owns things, rather it is more; because he owns and the action of another, while Allah does not own. And if ownership is established for Him over everything, then it is necessary to say that He created it; because the servant does not own it. And one owns things by having power over them or by being given ownership by the one who has that. His act, and there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This is a proof that God must be the creator of human actions. Since God is the owner (malik) of everything, and ownership implies either direct creation or receipt of ownership from another. Since God cannot receive ownership, He must be the creator. If He were not the owner of human acts, then servants would own their acts, and God's ownership would be deficient. Therefore, God creates human acts.

Key Terms
المالك
الملك
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's creation of all actions means humans have no free will or responsibility.
Correction: Maturidi's argument affirms God as ultimate creator, but human responsibility is preserved through acquisition (kasb). The passage does not address human freedom directly; it establishes the theological necessity of divine creation.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say God must be the creator of human actions?
A) Because the Quran says so
B) Because God is the owner of everything, and ownership implies creation
C) Because humans cannot act
D) Because the Mu'tazilites deny it

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that God's absolute ownership of everything logically necessitates that He is the Creator of all, including human actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is the true Owner of every action you perform, including your prayers and sins, how does that change the way you relate to your own deeds? Will you still claim them as 'mine' when they are good, or will you humbly acknowledge His creation? Before your next prayer, pause and say in your heart: 'This action is Yours, not mine.'
?
This lesson says 'there is no power except through Allah.' When was the last time you felt powerless and relied on your own strength? Commit this week to saying 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' (there is no power except through Allah) whenever you face a difficult action, and see if it changes your attitude.
?
Do you truly believe that your acts of worship are created by God? If so, why do you sometimes feel proud of them as if they were your own achievement? Write down one act of worship you are proud of and destroy the paper, symbolizing that it belongs to Allah, not you.

Substances Seen Through Accidents: Refuting Mu'tazila
Maturidi argues that substances are perceived through accidents, not essences, and uses this to refute Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that substances are perceived through their accidents, not directly, and how this refutes the Mu'tazili view on divine attributes.

Core Idea
We see things by their changes, not their essence.

Maturidi presents a subtle argument about perception: what we actually see are not the essences of substances but the accidents that change upon them. This observation becomes the basis for a powerful refutation of the Mu'tazili doctrine that God's attributes are not distinct from His essence. In this passage, he draws a parallel between created substances and the Creator's visibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Maturidi's concise argument. Pay attention to the flow from perception to theological conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key claim
Maturidi states that substances (jawahir) are not seen in their essence; they are seen through the change of accidents ('a'rad). Note this as the foundation.
3
Trace the analogical reasoning
Understand how Maturidi extends this principle: if created substances are not seen in themselves, then it is possible that similar things (like the Creator) are also not seen directly. This undermines the Mu'tazila.
4
Analyze the refutation
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila, by denying distinct attributes, align with atheists. Grasp why he sees this as a dangerous conclusion.
He ends with a prayer for deliverance from such a saying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيهم من لا نراه بجوهره وإن كان يرى ، وتلك الأفعال لأنفسها لا ترى ، إنما ترى وتعلم بتغير الأحوال على الجوهر ، فإذا كانت جواهر لا ترى جائز منها مثلها لم يجعل لما خلق علما ولا ذهب به فكيف ناقض به قول المعتزلة قول الملحدة وهم / شركاؤهم فى هذا الوجه ، فنسأل الله النجاة من قول هذا عقباه .
Among them are those whom we do not see by their essence, even if they are seen; those actions in themselves are not seen, they are only seen and known by the change of states upon the substance. So if substances are not seen, it is permissible that the like of them [not be seen]. He did not make for what He created knowledge nor did He go with it. So how can the saying of the Mu'tazila contradict the saying of the atheists, and they are partners in this aspect. So we ask Allah for deliverance from the consequence of this saying.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage argues that substances (jawahir) are not perceived directly in their essence; rather, they are known through the accidents ('a'rad) that change upon them. Maturidi uses this to show that the Mu'tazila, who deny that God's attributes are distinct from His essence, are actually in agreement with the atheists on this point—both deny the real visibility of things in themselves. The argument is that if the created substances are not seen in their essence, then by analogy, the Creator's essence is not seen either, but His attributes are manifest. This refutes the Mu'tazili claim that God's attributes are not additional to His essence.

Key Terms
جوهر (jauhar)
عرض ('arad)
رؤية (ru'ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies the possibility of seeing God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God is seen through His attributes, not His essence directly, similar to created substances.

Checkpoint

A) By their essence directly
B) Through the change of accidents upon them
C) By intellectual intuition
D) They are never perceived at all

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that substances are known through their accidents, not directly, and uses this to show that the Mu'tazili denial of distinct attributes aligns with atheism.

?

?
The passage ends with a prayer for deliverance from such a saying. Do you sincerely fear falling into a similar misguided belief? In what area of your faith might you be tempted to rationalize away a divine attribute?

God's Power is Complete, Not Deficient
Maturidi defines deficient power and argues for God's complete power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God's power cannot be deficient or limited like creaturely power.

Core Idea
God's power is absolute; creaturely power is inherently limited.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi draws a sharp contrast between the power of creatures and the power of God. He defines deficient power and uses it to show that God's power must be complete over all things, including the actions of His servants. This argument is foundational for understanding divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the concise style.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the Definition
Find the definition of deficient power (al-qudra al-naqisa). What is it? Who has it?
Maturidi says it belongs to every creature and each has power over some things but not others.
3
Follow the Logical Implication
See how Maturidi draws a conditional: if God lacked power over something His servant has power over, then God's power would be like deficient power. Understand why this is unacceptable.
This is an argument by contradiction.
4
Grasp the Conclusion
Maturidi concludes that God is exalted above such deficiency. Therefore, God must have power over all things, including human actions.
5
Reflect on Significance
Consider how this argument establishes God's absolute power and refutes any notion of independent creaturely power.
This connects to debates about free will and predestination (qadar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن القدرة الناقصة هى التى تكون لكل أحد من الخلق ، ولكل قدرة على ما ليس لغيره فإذا لم يكن الله قدرة على ما لعبده ، فإذا قدرته نحو قدرة كل منقوص ، جل الله عن صفة المخلوق ، وبالله التوفيق.
Indeed, deficient power is that which belongs to every one of the creation, and each has power over what is not for another. If then Allah does not have power over what His servant has, then His power would be like the power of every deficient one. Exalted is Allah above the attribute of the creature, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage defines deficient power (al-qudra al-naqisa) as the type of power that belongs to creatures: each creature has power over some things but not others. Maturidi draws a crucial theological implication: if God lacked power over something that His servant has power over, then God's power would be comparable to that of a deficient creature. This is impossible for Allah, as He is exalted above such deficiency. Therefore, God must have power over all things, including the actions of His servants.

Key Terms
قدرة ناقصة
قدرة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That creatures have independent power separate from God's power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that creaturely power is inherently deficient and limited; if God's power were like that, He would not be God. Thus, all power ultimately belongs to God.

Checkpoint

A) Absolute power
B) Deficient power
C) Acquired power
D) Essential power

Key Takeaway
God's power is absolute and encompasses all actions, unlike the limited power of creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you holding onto a sense of independence that actually makes your power deficient? Reflect on a recent event where you felt in control. How can you reframe it as an act of God's power?
?
If God's power encompasses even your own actions, how does that affect your fear or hope? Are you afraid of your own weakness or hopeful in His strength? Use this lesson to replace your fear with reliance on His complete power.

God's Absolute Power and the Credibility of His Promises
Maturidi argues that excluding anything from God's power undermines His promises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that denying God's power over any act undermines the credibility of His promises and threats.

Core Idea
If God lacks power over even one act, His promises become unreliable.

Maturidi presents a powerful reductio argument: if we allow that any act could be outside God's power, then we cannot trust His promises of reward, punishment, or resurrection. This reasoning shows that affirming God's absolute power is essential for the coherence of revelation. As you read, notice how he escalates the stakes from a single act to the entire structure of faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hypothetical
Notice Maturidi's opening conditional: 'if it were permissible for something...' This sets up the argument.
2
Trace the consequences
Follow the chain: if something were outside God's power, then promises become doubtful, and even resurrection becomes uncertain.
He uses rhetorical questions to drive the point.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Why does Maturidi bring up the mosquito and God's ability to create like? See how he contrasts immense power with a tiny creature to highlight absurdity.
This reinforces that any lack of power contradicts God's own statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه لوجاز خروج شيء هو تحت القدرة عن أن يكون الله عليه قدرة ، بل ليس هو شيئا واحدا ، بل لعله أكثر من جميع الخلق ، كيف نؤمن بوعده ووعيده ، وكيف يطمئن السامع إلى ما وعده من البعث أن يكون ، وما أخبر أنه لو شاء لخلق مثل الذى خلق ، وهو لا يقدر على فعل بعوض ، فضلا عن فعل هو أقوى منه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also, if it were permissible for something that is under power to come out such that Allah does not have power over it—nay, it is not one thing, but perhaps more than all of creation—how can we believe in His promise and His threat? And how can the listener be assured of what He promised of the resurrection, that it will be, and what He informed that if He willed He would create the like of what He created, while He is not able to do the act of a mosquito, let alone an act stronger than it? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument. Maturidi supposes the possibility that some thing (like a human act) might be outside God's power. He points out that this thing might not be one but many, perhaps more than all creation. If God lacked power over such things, then His promises of reward and punishment, and His ability to resurrect, would become doubtful. Moreover, God has told us that if He willed, He could create the like of what He created (implying His power is immense); yet if He cannot control even a mosquito's act, that would contradict His own claim. Thus, to preserve the credibility of divine revelation, one must affirm that God has power over all things, including human actions.

Key Terms
وعد (wa'd)
وعيد (wa'id)
بعث (ba'th)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power over human acts makes them compelled; trust in revelation does not require absolute power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if any act were outside God's power, even trust in His promises would be impossible. Affirming absolute power does not imply compulsion but secures the foundation of faith.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be jeopardized if any act were outside God's power?
A) God's knowledge
B) God's mercy
C) Trust in His promises and threats
D) The existence of the world

Key Takeaway
God's absolute power over all things is necessary for the reliability of His promises and threats.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a promise of God in the Quran that you secretly doubt will be fulfilled because you think it's too big or too hard? What does that doubt reveal about how you view God's power over even the smallest things? Today, pick one promise and write it down as a declaration of God's absolute power—then ask Him to help you live as if it's already true.
?
Consider a repeated sin in your life that feels impossible to overcome. Are you functionally believing that this habit is beyond God's power to change? If God can control the act of a mosquito, what's stopping you from trusting Him with this struggle? Commit to praying about that one sin today, specifically asking God to demonstrate His power in that area.
?
When you worry about the future—your health, provision, or loved ones—you are effectively denying God's power over those events. Which worry today is really a lack of trust in God's ability to handle the smallest detail? What step can you take right now to act on His promise to be sufficient?

God's Ownership Necessitates Creation of All Acts
Maturidi proves God must create all acts from His absolute ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's absolute ownership of everything logically entails that He creates all human acts.

Core Idea
Absolute ownership demands absolute creativity; no act is outside God's creation.

This lesson unpacks a key argument from Imam al-Maturidi that links God's attribute of ownership (milk) directly to His creative power. The argument is a response to those who would limit God's creative domain by exempting human acts. Maturidi shows that if God were not the owner and creator of all acts, His lordship would be imperfect, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the logical flow. Pay attention to the key terms ملك (ownership) and ربوبية (lordship).
The passage is dense; reading it twice helps.
2
Follow the argument chain
The argument goes: God is Owner by essence → if He were not Owner of acts, servants would be → that would make His ownership deficient → but it's not → therefore He is Owner → therefore He creates all acts.
Notice the conditional 'if not... then...' structure.
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on what this means for human responsibility. Maturidi does not deny human acquisition (kasb); rather, he affirms that God alone creates.
This argument is foundational for the Maturidi position on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله إذ هو مالك كل شيء وملكه الأشياء ليس بما أوجب له فيه الملك لملك العبد ، بل هو بذاته مالك بما هو خالق كل شيء . فأما أن يكون غير مالك لفعل العباد ولا رب ها فيجب به أن يكون للعباد ذلك ، فيكون ربوبيته وملكه ملكا ناقصا ، وذلك لكل مخلوق يملك أشياء ، بل هو أكثر ؛ لأنه يملك وفعل غيره والله لا [ يملك ] ، وإذا ثبت له الملك فى كل شيء لزم القول بخلقه ؛ إذ لا يملكه العبد ، ويملك الأشياء بالقدرة عليها أو تمليك من له تلك ، فعله ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also that Allah, since He is the Owner of everything and His ownership of things is not due to what obligates for Him ownership by the ownership of a servant, but rather He is Owner by His essence as the Creator of everything. So if He were not the Owner of the acts of servants and their Lord, then it would be necessary that the servants have that, and thus His lordship and ownership would be deficient ownership, and that is for every creature that owns things—nay, it is more, because He owns and the act of another, and Allah does not own it. And if ownership of everything is established for Him, then the statement of His creating it follows, since the servant does not own it, and he owns things by power over them or by transfer from one who has that. His act, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage argues from the nature of divine ownership (mulk). Maturidi states that Allah is the Owner of everything by virtue of His essence as Creator, not because anyone grants Him ownership. If He were not the Owner and Lord of the acts of servants, then servants would have to be the owners and lords of their own acts, which would imply a share in lordship. This would make God's ownership and lordship deficient, like that of a creature who owns some things. But God's ownership is absolute. Therefore, He must be the Owner of all acts, which entails that He creates them—since ownership implies the power to create or dispose. The servant cannot truly own his act because ownership by a creature is either by power (qudra) or by transfer (tamlik), neither of which applies to the servant's own act in an unearned way. Hence, God alone creates all acts.

Key Terms
ملك (milk)
ربوبية (rububiyya)
خالق (khaliq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies human free will and teaches that humans are compelled in all actions.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that God creates all acts, but humans acquire (kasb) them and are responsible. This argument does not negate human accountability; it establishes that creation (khalq) is exclusively God's, while acquisition (kasb) belongs to humans.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows from God's ownership of everything?
A) God must create everything
B) Humans are free to do as they wish
C) Ownership is irrelevant
D) God only owns some things

Key Takeaway
God's absolute ownership logically requires that He creates every act, including human actions; any other view implies a deficiency in His lordship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform—even your most private thoughts and intentions—is created by Allah? If so, how does that change the way you approach your next moment of weakness or sin? Commit to a specific situation today where you will pause and acknowledge Allah's ownership before acting.
?
The passage warns that denying God's ownership of our acts implies deficient lordship. Where in your life do you subtly act as if you are the independent owner of your deeds—your successes, your efforts, your choices? Identify one area and make a sincere du'a asking Allah to make you feel His absolute ownership in that area.
?
If Allah creates all acts, then what is your role? How does the Maturidi concept of kasb (acquisition) give you genuine responsibility while preserving Allah's sovereignty? Write down one resolution to consciously 'acquire' a good deed today with the awareness that Allah creates it, and reflect on how that changes your sense of reliance on Him.

Two Absurd Consequences if God Does Not Create Faith
Maturidi shows two absurdities if God did not create the act of faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two absurd consequences that arise if one denies that God creates the act of faith, and how these consequences prove that faith must be created by God.

Core Idea
Denying God's creation of faith leads to ranking the servant above God and undermining divine justice.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful argument against those who claim that humans create their own acts of faith, particularly the Mu'tazila. He shows that if God did not create the act of faith, two absurdities would follow: first, the believer would be considered superior to God in action, and second, the reward for faith would become disproportionate. By demonstrating the impossibility of these conclusions, Maturidi establishes that God alone is the Creator of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Let the argument unfold naturally before you analyze it.
Notice the strong language: 'أحسن الأفعال' (best actions) and 'أبينها' (clearest).
2
Identify the two absurdities
The passage lists two consequences if God does not create faith. Find each one and understand how Maturidi connects them to the broader argument.
The first is about superiority, the second about reward.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila connection
Maturidi specifically targets the Mu'tazila position. Notice how he ties the first absurdity to their view on the ugliness of disbelief.
This shows the argument is polemical as well as logical.
4
Trace the conclusion
After presenting the two absurdities, Maturidi concludes that the creation of faith as good must be from God. Ask yourself: How do the absurdities lead to this conclusion?
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is important for Islamic theology. What does it say about divine attributes and human responsibility?
This connects to broader debates on free will and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما إذ كان سبيل معرفة الفاعل إنما هو بأثار الفعل، ثم كان الإيمان من أحسن الأفعال في العقول وأنور الأشياء وأتمها وأجلها قدرا وأبينها لمرضاته، فلو قلنا: إن الله غير خالق له دخل علينا أمران في ذلك: أحدهما تفضيل من يطيع الله بالايمان وغيره على الله بما خلق من الأقذار والأنتان والخبائث والقبائح من الجواهر، مع ما كان ما حسن من الجواهر لا يبلغ قدر الذي ذكر من العبادات في الحسن والخير وإذا كان كذلك، ومعلوم تفاضل الفاضلين بتفاضل أفعالهم أوجب ذلك فضل العبد على الله في الفعل والخلق، وهذا بالمعتزلة أولى؛ لأنهم زعموا أن فعل الكفر قبيح شر من جميع الوجوه، وليس كذلك أمر القردة والخنازير، فمثله فعل الإيمان من جميع جواهر الحسان، والثانى أن ثوابه إذ حسنه حسى وحسن الإيمان عقلى وما حسن في الحس دون الذي يحسن فى العقل، إذ قد يجوز انقلاب مثله على ما مر بيانه، ولا يجوز انقلاب الآخر، وإذا كان كذلك فيقتصر الجزاء على قدر المجرى، والله وعد جزاء الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها، ثبت أن خلق فعل الإيمان حسنا الله، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Moreover, since the way to know the agent is only through the traces of the action, and faith is among the best actions in the intellects, the most luminous of things, the most complete, the most exalted in rank, and the clearest with respect to His pleasure. So if we say that God is not its Creator, two things would follow from that: one of them is the preference of one who obeys God by faith and other things over God, with respect to what He created of filth, stench, vile things and abominable substances, even though what is good from substances does not reach the degree of those acts of worship in goodness and virtue. And if that is the case, and it is known that the superiority of superior ones is due to the superiority of their actions, that would necessitate the superiority of the servant over God in action and creation. This applies more to the Mu'tazila because they claim that the act of disbelief is abominable in all respects, unlike the case of apes and pigs. So likewise the act of faith from all beautiful substances. The second [matter] is that the reward of [good actions], when their goodness is sensible, while the goodness of faith is intellectual, and what is good in the sensible is not like what is good in the intellectual, since it may be possible for the like of the former to change, as has been explained, while the latter cannot change. And if so, then recompense is limited to the extent of the worth. And God has promised reward for a good deed ten times its like. So it is established that the creation of the act of faith as good is from God, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 833

Commentary
This passage presents two absurd consequences that would follow if we maintained that God does not create the act of faith. The first consequence is that the servant who performs faith would be considered superior to God in terms of action and creation, because faith is the best of actions—more luminous, complete, and exalted than any physical creation of God, such as filth and vile substances. Since superiority is determined by actions, the servant would be better than God. This is even more problematic for the Mu'tazila, who hold that disbelief is worse than all vile things, but here the comparison is reversed. The second consequence concerns reward: sensible goods are mutable, while the goodness of faith is intellectual and immutable. Since reward is proportional to the worth of the act, and God promises tenfold reward for a good deed, it follows that the goodness of faith must be created by God, not by the servant. Thus, God is the Creator of the act of faith.

Key Terms
الإيمان (faith)
الفعل (action)
الخلق (creation)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)
الثواب (reward)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans create their own faith independently of God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if humans created faith, absurd consequences follow, so faith must be created by God.

Checkpoint
What is the first absurd consequence mentioned if God did not create the act of faith?
A) The servant would become a creator
B) The servant would be superior to God in action
C) Faith would become impossible
D) The reward for faith would be zero

Key Takeaway
The act of faith must be created by God; otherwise, absurd consequences about divine superiority and justice follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of faith—prayer, charity, or even a sincere thought—do you secretly credit yourself for it? How does Maturidi's argument force you to acknowledge God's role in every moment of obedience?
?
Maturidi says faith is the best, most luminous action. Is that how you treat your faith—as your highest priority? If not, what other 'actions' have you elevated above it, and what does that say about your understanding of God's creation?
?
The second absurdity ties reward to the immutable goodness of faith. If God creates every act of faith, then even your weakest attempt at belief is entirely His gift. Does that humble you or make you complacent? How will you respond to this truth tomorrow?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
God's Condemnation of Undeserved Praise Implies He Creates All Actions
Maturidi argues from God's condemnation of undeserved praise to His creating all actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses God's condemnation of undeserved praise to prove that God is the Creator of all actions, including acts of faith and gratitude.

Core Idea
God cannot condemn what He Himself does—so He must create the actions He praises.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise logical argument rooted in a Qur'anic verse. He observes that God condemns those who love to be praised for what they have not done. Yet God commands His servants to thank Him for faith and praise Him for blessings. If God were not the Creator of these actions, He would be seeking praise for what He did not do—exactly what He condemns. This contradiction leads to the conclusion that God must be the Creator of all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Start by reading the original Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Focus on understanding the flow of the argument without overanalyzing.
Notice the key terms: الحمد, الشكر, الإيمان, الأنعام.
2
Identify the Two Parts
The passage has two main components: God's condemnation of those who love undeserved praise, and His command that servants thank and praise Him. Identify each part.
The condemnation is based on Qur'an 3:188.
3
Understand the Logical Inference
Maturidi argues that if God were not the Creator of the acts for which He demands praise, He would be like those He condemns—seeking praise for what He did not do. This would be a contradiction.
God cannot contradict His own judgment, so He must be the Creator.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
The conclusion is that God must be the Creator of all actions, including the acts of faith (إيمان) and gratitude (شكر) that He commands.
This reinforces the Maturidi doctrine of God as the sole Creator of all actions.
5
Test Your Understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to solidify your understanding and ensure you can reproduce the argument.
The question is based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن الله تعالى ذم الذين قالوا: وتحبون أن يحمدوا بما لم يفعلوا، ثم ألزم عباده الشكر له على الإيمان، والحمد لله على الأنعام، لم يجز يكون غير خالق لذلك فيستأدى الحمد على ما لم يفعله، والشكر على ما لم يسد إلى أحد به، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And furthermore, God Most High has condemned those who said, 'And you love to be praised for what you have not done.' Then He made incumbent upon His servants thankfulness to Him for faith, and praise to God for blessings. It is not permissible that He is not the Creator of that, such that He would claim praise for what He did not do, and thankfulness for what He did not confer upon anyone. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage offers a straightforward argument: God condemns those who love to be praised for things they did not do. At the same time, He commands His servants to thank Him for faith and to praise Him for blessings. If God were not the Creator of these acts of faith and gratitude, then He would be exactly like those He condemned—seeking praise for something He did not do. Since that is impossible, it follows that God must be the Creator of all actions, including the acts of faith and gratitude.

Misconception: The argument is about human free will and whether humans create their own actions.
Correction: This argument is not about human agency; it is about God's consistency. Maturidi uses God's condemnation of undeserved praise to show that God must be the Creator of the actions for which He asks praise, otherwise He would be doing what He condemns.

Key Takeaway

The Absurdity of Mu'tazila on Power
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila by showing their concept of power leads to absurdity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical absurdity in the Mu'tazila's view of human power and why Maturidi insists that God is the Creator of all actions.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila's view makes the servant more powerful than God.

Imam Maturidi presents a sharp critique of the Mu'tazila's concept of power (qudra). He argues that if human action is defined as origination from nothing, just like God's action, then assigning a power of acquisition to the servant while denying it to God leads to an absurdity: the servant becomes more powerful than God. This passage exposes the logical consequences and reaffirms that God alone creates all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument; note the key terms.
The Arabic has some uncertain phrases but the argument is clear.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's position
Find how Maturidi describes the Mu'tazila's view: they equate God's act with the servant's act, and assign power of acquisition to the servant but not to God.
3
Trace the absurd consequence
Notice that Maturidi argues that this makes the servant greater in power, because his power applies to diverse actions, while God's power is supposedly limited. Then he points out that the Mu'tazila give the servant power to prevent the Lord.
The exact phrase 'على منع الرب عن ينفى [عنه] الحيرة' is garbled but the meaning is clear.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that both reason and revelation reject this, so it is established that God is the Creator of all actions.
This is a key Ash'ari/Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن معنى فعل الله هو الإبداع والإخراج من العدم إلى الوجود، وصيرت المعتزلة ذلك معنى فعل العبد، ثم جعلت للعبد قدرة على الكسب، ولم تجعل الله، فصار العبد بذلك أعظم في القدرة؛ إذ هي تقع على مختلف الأمر من الله؛ إذ قدرته ترجع إلى أحد الوجهين، ومما يبين أن كل شيء فعله نوع جعلوه طباعا، ومن كان فعلين جعلوه أخيارا عن قدرة، فيجب في الأول كذلك، وذلك هو الحق عند المعتزلة؛ لأنهم يجعلون للعبد قدرة على منع الرب عن ينفى [عنه] الحيرة، ولا يجعلون مثله لله إلا أن يذهب عنه قدرة العبد، وإذا ثبت أن فى نفى خلق الأفعال تحقيق ذلك وذلك مما يأباه العقل والسمع جميعا - ثبت أن الله خالق الأفعال كلها، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Also, the meaning of the act of God is origination (ibda') and bringing out from non-existence into existence. The Mu'tazila made that the meaning of the act of the servant, then they assigned to the servant a power (qudra) of acquisition (kasb), and did not assign it to God. So the servant became greater in power by that, since it applies to different matters from God, for His power is limited to one of two aspects. And from what shows that everything is an act, they made it a type of nature (tab'), and they made two acts as voluntary (ikhtiyari) from power. So it is necessary in the first likewise, and that is the truth according to the Mu'tazila, because they assign to the servant a power to prevent the Lord from [?], and they do not assign the like to God unless the power of the servant disappears. And when it is established that in negating the creation of actions lies the realization of that, and that is repudiated by both reason and revelation, it is established that God is the Creator of all actions, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazila's distinction between divine creative power and human acquired power. The author argues that the Mu'tazila have equated the meaning of God's action (origination from nothing) with the servant's action, but then they assign to the servant a power of acquisition (kasb) that they deny to God. As a result, the servant becomes greater in power because his power can produce diverse actions, whereas God's power is supposedly restricted. The author points out that the Mu'tazila even go so far as to attribute to the servant a power that can prevent God from acting, which is absurd. Since reason and revelation both reject the negation of God's creation of actions, it is clear that God creates all actions.

Key Terms
الإبداع (origination)
الكسب (acquisition)
القدرة (power)
الطباع (nature)
الاختيار (volition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about free will vs. determinism.
Correction: Maturidi is not directly arguing about free will but about the logical consequences of the Mu'tazila's definition of power. He shows that their view leads to making the servant more powerful than God.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Maturidi point out in the Mu'tazila's view of power?
A) The servant has no power at all
B) The servant has power to prevent God's action
C) God gives all power to the servant
D) The Mu'tazila deny God's existence

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's view of human power leads to the absurd consequence that the servant can prevent God's action, so it must be rejected; God alone creates all actions.
