امتداح
تخصيص
وكيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible to include God in the names of created beings, or to say God is like a scholar, agent, lord, etc.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God is absolutely transcendent and cannot be included in any created name because it would contradict His unique attributes of being the Lord, Trustee, and God of all, and would nullify the praise due to Him.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the second argument (الوجه الثاني) against including God in names?
A) It nullifies knowledge of the intended meaning.
B) It would cause the praise to fall because praise is for making everything under power.
C) It implies God is like other things.
D) It is impossible in reason.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's three arguments establish that God is utterly transcendent and cannot be included in any category of created names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What concrete step can you take today to mentally separate God's lordship from all earthly authority?
?
You often refer to God as 'the Lord of everything' in your prayers. But do you truly live as if nothing is outside His dominion—including your time, your plans, your relationships? Where in your daily routine do you act as if something is 'excluded' from His authority? What would change if you took the first argument literally?
?
Maturidi says that any statement implying 'other than me' regarding God's actions is invalid. When you proudly say 'I did this' or 'I achieved that', do you realize you are implying that some action originates from other than God? How can you rephrase your thoughts and speech to reflect that all actions return to Him?

Four Aspects of Divine Praise and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Maturidi details four aspects of divine praise and refutes Mu'tazila claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four aspects through which true praise of God is established, and how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila's flawed attribution of praise.

Core Idea
God's praise is unique, rooted in His absolute power and independence from creation.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi delves into the nature of divine praise, arguing that true praise stems from specific aspects that highlight God's sovereignty and the creature's utter dependence. He also critiques the Mu'tazila for attributing praise to God in a manner that compromises His transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the refutation of the Mu'tazila at the beginning.
2
Identify the four aspects of praise
Look for the four 'وجوه' (aspects) that Maturidi lists. Understand each one's basis in God's attributes.
The list starts with 'أحدها' (first) and continues.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
See how Maturidi counters the Mu'tazila claim that praise can be attributed to God based on His creation.
Focus on the final part about the instability of that attribution.
4
Reflect on God's transcendence
Consider the conclusion that God's glory is permanent and free from interruption, unlike created praise.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasped the fourth aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن الآية لو نزلت فيهم لنزلت في ذمهم ، ووصف فيما نفوا عن الله من الوجه الذي نفى أهل الاعتزال ، فذلك لازم لهم ، مع أن الآية لا يعمل بها المعتزلة من ذلك الوجه الذى فى أصل دينهم جواز إضافة حقيقة ذلك إلى الله ، فكيف يجتمع على منكر مثله ممن يزعم أن ذلك في العقل مدفوع ، وطريقة السمع . ومحال الاحتجاج بالسمع على إمكانه فى العقل ، ثبت أن حقيقة ذلك في أفعال الخلق ، وبه يكون امتداح فى الحقيقة من وجوه : أحدها في جعل كل شيء بحيث القدرة تحت قدرة الله ؛ ليظهر حاجة الخلق جملة إلى الله تعالى في كون كل شيء لهم به . والثانى أن الوصف بالقدرة على ما لا فعل لغيره ليس بعجيب ، بل يستحقه كل ضعيف مهان ، ثبت أن الامتداح يكون من هذا الوجه . والثالث فيه بيان سفه من يفهم أن خلف كل شيء على ما عليه يوجب وصف الرب ، أو تحقيق الفعل من الوجه الذى يكون من العباد منه . والرابع ليعلم أن الله يتعالى أن يلحقه ذم فى فعل أو مدح من حيث ذلك المفعول بحال ، وذلك ببعض الاعتزال ؛ إذ جعلوا له هذا الامتداح بخلقه ، وما يكون كذلك فهو شرف الدوام وخوف الانقطاع ، جل ربنا عن ذلك .
And then, if the verse had been revealed concerning them, it would have been revealed in their blame, and a description of what they denied about God from the aspect that the people of i'tizal denied; so that is binding upon them, even though the Mu'tazila do not act upon the verse from that aspect because in their religion it is permissible to attribute the reality of that to God. So how can one who denies such a thing—who claims that it is repudiated in reason and the method of transmission—agree? And it is impossible to argue by transmission for its possibility in reason. It is established that the reality of that is in the actions of creatures, and thereby praise is in reality from aspects: first, in making everything so that power is under the power of God, to show the need of all creatures towards God Almighty in the existence of everything for them. Second, describing with power over what no other can do is not wonderful, rather every weak and despised person deserves it, so it is established that praise arises from this aspect. Third, it contains an explanation of the foolishness of one who thinks that everything being as it is necessitates a description of the Lord, or the realization of the action from the aspect that it is from servants. Fourth, to know that God is exalted above being subject to blame in an action or praise from the aspect of that object in any state; and that is in some of the Mu'tazila, for they attributed this praise to Him through His creation, and what is like that is the honor of permanence and the fear of cessation—our Lord is exalted above that.
Source pages p. 845

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and classification. Maturidi argues that if a particular verse had been revealed against the Mu'tazila, it would have condemned them. He points out that the Mu'tazila themselves do not act on certain verses because their doctrine permits attributing reality to God, which is inconsistent. He then establishes that true praise of God consists in four aspects: (1) Making everything subject to God's power to show creation's need. (2) Describing God with power over what no other can do—even a weak person can claim power over his own actions, so this praise is unique. (3) Exposing the foolishness of those who think that the state of things necessitates describing God in human terms. (4) God is beyond any blame or praise based on the mere object of action; the Mu'tazila wrongly attribute praise to God based solely on His creation, which is unstable. Maturidi concludes that true glory is permanent and cannot be interrupted.

Key Terms
امتداح
قدرة
اعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power is like human power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that God's power is absolutely unique: He has power over all things, whereas even a weak person can claim power over his own actions. True divine praise is based on His exclusive power and independence, not on any resemblance to creatures.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth aspect of praise (الوجه الرابع), what does Maturidi say about God's relation to praise and blame?
A) God is subject to praise based on His actions.
B) God is exalted above any blame or praise based on the object of action.
C) God's praise depends on His creation.
D) God's attributes are like those of creatures.

Key Takeaway
True praise of God is based on His absolute power and the perpetual need of all creation, not on any attribute resembling creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson teaches that true praise is rooted in His absolute power and your complete need. Before your next prayer, pause and recite 'Alhamdulillah' while intentionally detaching it from any blessing you've received—focus solely on His perfection.
?
The Mu'tazila thought God's glory depends on His creation. In your own life, do you ever feel that your acts of worship add something to God? How would your heart change if you deeply believed that He is exalted above any increase or decrease from your obedience? Commit today to perform one good deed purely to acknowledge His transcendence, not to seek any return.

The Four Aspects of Divine Praise in Creation
Maturidi enumerates four aspects of divine praise through creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four aspects through which God's praise arises from His creation, according to Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
God's praise comes from His making all things dependent on Him, not from sheer power.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing that revealed evidence cannot be used to prove what reason deems impossible. He then asserts that the true basis for praising God lies in His actions of creation. From this, he derives four distinct aspects of praise, each highlighting a different dimension of God's majesty and refuting misguided views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a principle followed by an enumeration of four aspects.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Identify the four aspects
Find the four numbered aspects (أحدها, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع). Understand each one's main point.
3
Connect each aspect to the central idea
Ask how each aspect shows God's praise is rooted in making creation dependent on Him.
This helps see the unity of Maturidi's argument.
4
Reflect on the refutation of Mu'tazila
In the fourth aspect, Maturidi explicitly criticizes the Mu'tazila. Understand why their view is problematic.
He says their view implies temporality and cessation, which is impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحال الاحتجاج بالسمع على إمكانه في العقل، ثبت أن حقيقة ذلك في أفعال الخلق، وبه يكون امتداح في الحقيقة من وجوه: أحدها في جعل كل شيء بحيث القدرة تحت قدرة الله؛ ليظهر حاجة الخلق جملة إلى الله تعالى في كون كل شيء لهم به. والثاني أن الوصف بالقدرة على ما لا فعل لغيره ليس بعجيب، بل يستحقه كل ضعيف مهان، ثبت أن الامتداح يكون من هذا الوجه. والثالث فيه بيان سفه من يفهم أن خلف كل شيء على ما عليه يوجب وصف الرب، أو تحقيق الفعل من الوجه الذي يكون من العباد منه. والرابع ليعلم أن الله يتعالى أن يلحقه ذم في فعل أو مدح من حيث ذلك المفعول بحال، وذلك ببعض الاعتزال؛ إذ جعلوا له هذا الامتداح بخلقه، وما يكون كذلك فهو شرف الدوام وخوف الانقطاع، جل ربنا عن ذلك.
And it is impossible to argue by revelation (sam') for its possibility in reason. It is established that the reality of that is in the actions of creation, and by it there is praise in reality from aspects: One: in making everything such that power is under the power of God, to show the need of all creation to God Almighty in that everything is for them. The second: that the description of power over what no other does is not marvelous, rather every weak and contemptible one deserves it; it is established that the praise is from this aspect. The third: in it is an explanation of the foolishness of one who understands that the succession of everything as it is necessitates describing the Lord, or the actualization of the action from the aspect that is from servants. The fourth: to know that God is exalted above being subject to blame in an action or praise from that object in any state, and that by some of the Mu'tazila; for they made this praise for Him by His creation, and whatever is like that is the honor of permanence and fear of cessation, exalted is our Lord above that.
Source pages p. 845

Commentary
This passage is a proof and enumeration. Maturidi first states a fundamental principle: it is impossible to use revealed evidence (sam') to prove the rational possibility of something that reason deems impossible. He then asserts that the true reality of the matter under discussion lies in the actions of creation. From this, praise (imtidaah) of God arises in four aspects. First aspect: God's making everything dependent on His power, so that all creation's need for Him is manifest. Second aspect: having power over what no one else does is not remarkable; even the weakest being has power over its own actions, so true praise must come from something else. Third aspect: it shows the foolishness of thinking that the succession of events obligates describing God in a certain way or that actions are actualized from the same aspect as human actions. Fourth aspect: it makes known that God is far above being praised or blamed based on the object of the action; the Mu'tazila mistakenly attribute this kind of praise to God through His creation, but that would imply permanence and fear of cessation, which is impossible for God. Thus, Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
السمع
العقل
امتداح
أفعال الخلق
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's praise comes from His sheer power alone, independent of creation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that true praise arises from making all creation dependent on Him, not from mere power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the first aspect of praise from the actions of creation?
A) God's power over all things
B) God's making everything dependent on His power to show creation's need
C) God's creation of evil
D) God's independence from creation

Key Takeaway
God's true praise arises from His making all creation dependent on Him, not from mere power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says that even the weakest being has power over its own actions, so power alone is not praiseworthy. What does this say about your own tendency to boast in your abilities? Where in your life are you falsely taking pride in what you do apart from God's constant provision?
?
The Mu'tazila mistakenly thought God's praise comes from His creation in a way that implies fear of cessation. Are there moments when you fear that God's praise or your relationship with Him could end? How does this lesson call you to trust in His eternal nature?

Refutation of the Interpretation of 'Allah created you and what you do'
Maturidi refutes the opponent's proof from the verse and clarifies its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists that the apparent meaning of 'what you do' in verse 37:96 refers to the creation of actions themselves, not the objects of those actions.

Core Idea
The verse creates actions, not objects—a key distinction in refuting anthropomorphic interpretations.

Imam Maturidi now tackles a critical proof used by opponents who interpret 'what you do' as referring to the idols or objects of human actions. By analyzing the verse's apparent meaning and parallel Quranic passages, he shows that the text is about the creation of the very act of doing, not the things made. This distinction is essential for preserving the uniqueness of divine creative power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice that Maturidi presents the opponent's view before his own refutation.
The passage ends mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of Maturidi's second point.
2
Identify the opponent's proof
The opponent uses verse 37:96 and parallels like 37:95 and 20:69 to argue that 'what you do' means the carved idols. Understand this claim.
3
Analyze Maturidi's first response
Maturidi says the apparent meaning (zaahir) of the verse is creation of the action (خلق العمل). He argues that it cannot be diverted to another meaning without evidence.
He also notes that the blame is on the action of carving, not the carved object.
4
Understand the second argument
Maturidi adds a logical point: if the verse is interpreted as creating objects (ma'mool), then if God did not create the action, one cannot say He created the object as a product of that action.
This part is incomplete but the logic is clear.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that 'what you do' refers to idols. Maturidi corrects this by insisting on the literal sense of creating the act itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج لخصمه بقوله: والله خلقكم وما تعملون، قال: يريد به، كقوله: (أتعبدون ما تنحتون)، وكقوله: (تلقف ما يأفكون). قال الشيخ رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق ظاهر الآية ذكر خلق العمل، فلم يجز صرف ذلك إلى غيره إلا بالبيان، مع ما في جميع ما ذكر نحتهم داخل، وكذلك إفكهم ما ذكر وبه عوبوا لا بذلك الشيء حيث فعلوا ثم عبدوا، فكأنهم عبدوا فعلهم فمثله ما نحن فيه. أيضا أنه لو شرح بالآية آلهتهم ذكر معمولا، فإذا لم يكن الله خلق العمل لم يجز له القول بخلقه معمولا؛ إذ
Then he argued for his opponent by His statement: 'And Allah created you and what you do.' He said: He means by it, like His statement: 'Do you worship what you carve?' and like His statement: 'It swallows what they faked.' The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: the apparent meaning of the verse mentions the creation of the action, so it is not permissible to divert that to other than it except by evidence. And with that, all that is mentioned of their carving is included, and likewise their faking, as mentioned, and by it they were reviled, not by that thing where they did then worshipped. So it is as if they worshipped their own action, so similar is what we are in. Also, if he explained by the verse that their gods are mentioned as an object, then if God did not create the action, it is not permissible for him to say that He created it as an object; because...
Source pages p. 846

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Maturidi presents the opponent's proof from the Quranic verse 'Allah created you and what you do' (37:96). The opponent claims that 'what you do' refers to the objects of actions, i.e., the idols they worshipped. Then the Shaykh (Maturidi) responds. He says the apparent meaning (zaahir) of the verse is the creation of the action itself ('amal), not the object. Therefore, it cannot be turned away from that meaning without clear evidence. He then argues that all the things mentioned, such as their carving and faking, are included, but the blame is on the action of carving, not on the carved object. They worshipped their own actions, similar to the issue at hand. Then he adds another point: if someone interprets the verse to mean that the gods are the created objects (ma'mool), then if God did not create the action, it would not be permissible to say He created the object as a product of the action, because that would imply creation of the action itself. The passage ends mid-sentence, probably continuing the refutation.

Key Terms
ظاهر الآية
خلق العمل
المعمول
عبادة الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'what you do' refers to the idols that people worship, making God the creator of those objects.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the apparent meaning is creation of the action itself; the parallel verses blame the action, not the object.

Checkpoint

A) The objects that are made
B) The actions themselves
C) The intentions
D) The materials used

Key Takeaway
Maturidi insists that 'what you do' refers to the creation of human actions, not the objects, preserving the doctrine that God creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is one specific act of worship you need to consciously attribute back to Him?

The Absurdity of Independent Human Power
Maturidi refutes independent human power via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing independent power to humans implies they could thwart God's plans, leading to an absurd reversal of roles.

Core Idea
Independent human power would make God dependent on human cooperation.

Maturidi continues his refutation of dualism by exposing the logical consequences of claiming humans have independent power. Such a claim, he argues, would allow humans to nullify God's governance and even place God in their debt. This passage lays out the reductio ad absurdum that shows why only God has ultimate power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the conditional logic: 'if humans had power, then...'
The passage is a single argument; let the language sink in.
2
Identify the hypothetical consequence
Find the first consequence: humans could nullify the governance of the world. Then see how Maturidi extends this to messengers and each person.
3
Understand the underlying assumption
The argument is built on the idea that if humans have independent power, God cannot compel them. Maturidi shows this undermines God's authority.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the final verdict
Maturidi calls this 'the most awful statement.' Realize that this shows the necessity of denying independent human power to preserve tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى قول هؤلاء يكون للبشر قدرة نقض تدبير العالم، وللرسل قوة أن لا يظهروا الله حجة في الأرض، وأن يمتنع كل منهم عن الوجه الذي عليه مضى تدبير، وهو مبنى على كون أحواله على أيدي البشر وخلق الأرض والسماء، والله تعالى لم يكن له قدرة على خلق تلك الأفعال والأحوال على أيديهم، ولهم قدرة على أن لا يفعلوا شيئا من ذلك، فإذا لهم عليه أرفع المن وأعلى النعم؛ إذ على القدرة في منع تقديره ونفاذ تدبيره فعلوا الذي به قام تدبيره وتم ملكه وسلطانه وعلى ما دبر وشاء، وذلك أوحش قول وبالله التوفيق.
And according to the statement of these (dualists), humans would have the power to nullify the governance of the world, and the messengers would have the power not to manifest God's proof on earth, and each of them could refrain from the course upon which governance proceeded. This is built upon the idea that His affairs are in the hands of humans, and the creation of the earth and sky [is in their hands?], and Allah, may He be exalted, had no power to create those actions and states through them, while they have the power not to do any of that. Then they would have upon Him the most elevated favor and the highest grace; for, in spite of their power to prevent His decree and the execution of His governance, they did that by which His governance stood and His kingdom and authority were completed, and upon what He decreed and willed. That is the most awful statement. And success is through Allah.
Source pages p. 859

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the refutation of dualism, specifically attacking the notion that humans have independent power. Maturidi argues that if humans had such power, they could nullify God's governance of the world. Even the prophets could choose not to manifest God's proof. This would imply that God's plans depend on human will—an absurdity. Moreover, if humans refrain from action, they would be withholding favor from God, because God's kingdom would only be completed through their voluntary actions. This elevates humans above God, which is an 'awful statement'. Maturidi concludes that this is utterly repugnant.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir)
قدرة (qudra)
نقض (naqd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think human free will means total independence from God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that total independence would allow humans to thwart God's plans, which is impossible; therefore human power is always subordinate to God's.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be a direct result if humans had independent power?
A) Humans would be more grateful to God
B) Humans could nullify God's governance
C) Humans would become prophets
D) Humans would create the earth

Key Takeaway
Attributing independent power to humans leads to the absurd conclusion that humans can thwart God's governance and put God in their debt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider the weight of the statement that independent human power would put God in our debt. Have you ever felt that you were doing God a favor by worshipping or obeying Him? How does this lesson expose that feeling as an absurdity, and what can you do to replace it with humble gratitude?

Two Methods of Reasoning from Witnessed to Unseen
Maturidi critiques two methods of analogical reasoning from shahid to gha'ib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of two methods of analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen and why both methods fail.

Core Idea
Analogy from seen to unseen must be based on meaning, not superficial resemblance.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi examines how opponents might try to argue from the created world to the divine. He identifies two possible approaches—direct analogy of human categories and consideration of underlying meaning—and demonstrates that neither can stand. This analysis reveals the importance of proper reasoning in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice how Maturidi structures his critique around two options.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two methods
Find the two methods Maturidi presents: (1) judging everything by human wisdom/foolishness, and (2) considering the underlying meaning that makes something what it is.
These are introduced with 'إما أن يجعل...' and 'أو ينظر...'.
3
Examine the refutation of the first method
Notice the absurd consequences Maturidi lists: creating without benefit, creating from nothing, punishment without cause, and permitting lying. This shows the first method leads to theological problems.
4
Examine the refutation of the second method
Understand why the second method is invalid: it relies on 'intuition of reason' (badahat al-aql), which is not a sound basis and is dismissed as baseless fantasy.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Maturidi's critique matters for tawhid. It shows that reasoning from creation to God must be done carefully, avoiding simplistic analogies.
This connects to the broader methodology of Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا بخروجه ذلك وتعنته فما ادعى ، على أنه لا يخلو من أحد أمرين : إما أن يجعل كل شيء يعرفه فى الشاهد البشر حكمة أو سفها يقول به فى الغائب ، أو ينظر إلى المعنى الذي له صار كذلك من في التحقيق فنقول به فى الغائب، فإن قال بالأول لزمه ذلك في خلق ما لا ينتفع به ، وفى خلق الشيء من لا شيء ، وفى التعذيب من غير دفع ، ثم يقال نفسه في إجازته قوله بالكذب ، فمها أجاب من شيء فذلك لازم له فيما قال ، وإن نظر إلى المعنى أبطل قوله ذا يبديهة العقل ، وذا لا يجوز له ، وهذا النوع من الخيال الذي لا أصل له فلا
And we have shown through his deviation and stubbornness what he claimed, that he is not free from one of two matters: either he makes everything he knows in the witnessed (al-shahid) of man wisdom or foolishness and says it about the unseen (al-gha'ib), or he considers the meaning by which it became so from verification and say it about the unseen. If he says the first, then it necessitates for him in creating what is not beneficial, and in creating a thing from nothing, and in punishment without repelling, then he says himself in permitting his statement of lying, so whatever he answers from something, that is necessary for him regarding what he said. And if he considers the meaning, he invalidates his statement by intuition of reason (badahat al-aql), and that is not permissible for him. And this type of fantasy that has no foundation, so no.
Source pages p. 866

Commentary
This passage is a critique of two possible methods of analogical reasoning from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). Maturidi states that any opponent must adopt one of two approaches: either they judge everything in the unseen by direct analogy of wisdom or foolishness as seen in humans, or they consider the underlying meaning (al-ma'na) that makes a thing what it is in the witnessed and then apply that to the unseen. He then refutes both. The first leads to absurd consequences: it would necessitate that God creates things without benefit, creates from nothing, and punishes without cause, and it also forces the opponent to permit lying (al-kadhib). Whatever answer they give, it remains binding. The second approach is invalid because it relies on 'intuition of reason' (badahat al-aql), which is not a valid basis for judgment—such intuitive claims are baseless fantasy. Maturidi thus shows that neither method can stand.

Key Terms
الشاهد (al-shahid)
الغائب (al-gha'ib)
الحكمة (al-hikma)
السفه (al-safah)
بديهة العقل (badahat al-aql)
المعنى (al-ma'na)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen is always valid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that it must be carefully critiqued; both naive analogy and appeal to underlying meaning are flawed.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two possible methods for reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen?
A) Analogy of essence and analogy of accident
B) Analogy of wisdom/foolishness and consideration of underlying meaning
C) Analogy of benefit and harm
D) Analogy of power and weakness

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that neither direct analogy nor intuitive meaning can justify reasoning from creation to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or see suffering in the world, do you find yourself quietly measuring God's wisdom by your own? How does this passage expose that tendency as absurd?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something makes sense to your intuition, it must be true about God? Maturidi calls such intuition a baseless fantasy. What assumptions about God do you hold that you have never critically examined?
?
This passage forces you to choose: will you continue to project your own ideas of wisdom and foolishness onto God, or will you submit to the realization that God's ways are beyond your comprehension? What practical step can you take this week to guard against such projection?

Refutation of the Objection on Creating Non-Beneficial Things
Maturidi rebuts the objection that creating non-beneficial things is ugly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's creation of non-beneficial things does not entail ugliness or obligation, and that ugliness is not intrinsic to actions like obscenities and disbelief.

Core Idea
Ugliness is not intrinsic; benefit is not incumbent upon the Creator.

This passage addresses a key objection: if God creates things with no apparent benefit, does that imply ugliness? Maturidi refutes this by showing that the opponent's appeal to 'intuition of reason' is unfounded, and that what is considered ugly (like obscenities and disbelief) is not ugly in itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the argument. Note the flow from objection to refutation.
2
Identify the main objection and response
What is the objection? It is about creating non-beneficial things. How does Maturidi respond? He says it does not entail obligation.
3
Examine the intuition of reason claim
Why does Maturidi say the opponent's claim is false? Because they are only using analogy, not pure intuition.
4
Understand the nature of ugliness
What does Maturidi conclude about ugly actions? They are not ugly in themselves. This is a key theological point.
5
Reflect on benefit and harm
How does the concept of benefit relate to wisdom? Actions not beneficial to themselves are not described as wisdom; but insistence on benefit can become harmful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تم دفع ما عورض بخلق شيء لا ينتفع به بما لم يدل ذلك على تكليف . الزمين ، فهذا يبين أن الذى قال ببديهة العقل كذب ، وأنه إنما ادعى القياس . على ما وافقه خصمه عليه لا غير ، ثم حصل بما عارض به خصمه مما ادعى ببديهة العقل على من يثبت صدقا مما هو كذب في الشاهد . ثم أجاب بما اختراز النفع بالفعل لا يحق عليه ، ثبت أن ما قبح منه لم يقبح لعينه ، وكذلك يجد خصمه مما يقدر عليه لا يجوز التكليف به ، ثبت أن ذلك لم يقبح لنفسه نحو الفواحش والكفر ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . يوجود وذا أولى لما يجئ مثل هذا من الصغار لا يلحقه وصف فحش ولا كفر ، ولا يجئ منهم فعل غير نافع نفسه يوصف بالحكمة ، والذى قال من إصرار النفع فهو من ذلك الوجه حكمة ، ولكن من وجه الضرر صار كذلك ، وهو ضرر العاقبة أو كفران النعمة أو مخالفة الرب في الفعل. وإذا كان به دفعه ولزمه السؤال ولم يلزم خصمه فيما عارضه بالزمن الذى أجابه مما ينقض عليه ليعلم به بعده عن الحق فيها يوافق عليه ويخالف فيه جميعا . ثم ان جواب خصمه سهل ، وهو ما بينا ، والله أعلم .
Then was refuted what was objected regarding creating something not beneficial, by that not indicating obligation upon the two times [؟]. This clarifies that the one who said by intuition of reason (badahat al-aql) lied, and that he only claimed analogy upon what his opponent agreed with him, nothing else. Then he obtained by what he opposed his opponent from what he claimed by intuition of reason against one who affirms truth from what is false in the witnessed. Then he answered by selecting benefit by the act is not incumbent upon him. It is established that what is ugly from it is not ugly in itself, and likewise his opponent finds from what he has power over that obligation is not permissible by it. It is established that that is not ugly in itself, like obscenities (al-fawahish) and disbelief (al-kufr). And there is no power except with God. And it exists, and this is more appropriate for what comes like this from small actions that do not admit description of obscenity nor disbelief, and no action not beneficial to itself comes from them that is described as wisdom. And what he said of insistence on benefit is from that aspect wisdom, but from the aspect of harm it becomes thus, and it is harm of the outcome or ingratitude for blessing or disobedience to the Lord in action. And if it is by that, he refutes it and the question is necessary for him, and it is not necessary for his opponent in what he opposed him with the time he answered, from what invalidates his argument, so that by it he knows his distance from the truth in what he agrees upon and differs in all. Then the answer of his opponent is easy, and it is what we have shown, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning God's creation of non-beneficial things. Maturidi argues that such creation does not entail any obligation (taklif) on God. This shows that the opponent who claims 'intuition of reason' is lying; they are merely claiming analogy based on what their opponent has already accepted. The opponent attempts to use intuitive reason to establish that something true in the witnessed is false, but Maturidi counters that the benefit of an act is not necessarily incumbent upon the agent. It is then established that what is ugly (qabih) from God is not ugly in its essence (li-aynihi), just as the opponent himself finds that obligation is not permissible for what he has power over—thus obscenities (fawahish) and disbelief (kufr) are not ugly in themselves. Maturidi further notes that small actions that are not classed as obscene or disbelief and that are not beneficial to themselves are not described as wisdom. The claim of 'insistence on benefit' is wisdom from one aspect but becomes harmful from another—harm of outcome, ingratitude, or disobedience. The opponent's objection is refuted, and the answer is straightforward.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل (badahat al-aql)
التكليف (al-taklif)
القبيح بعينه (al-qabih bi-aynihi)
الفواحش (al-fawahish)
الكفر (al-kufr)
الحكمة (al-hikma)
الضرر (al-darar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creating non-beneficial things is intrinsically ugly and thus impossible for God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that ugliness is not intrinsic; what is ugly in human context (like obscenities) is not ugly in itself. God's creation of non-beneficial things does not entail obligation or ugliness.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about actions that are considered ugly, such as obscenities and disbelief?
A) They are ugly in themselves (li-aynihi)
B) They are not ugly in themselves
C) They are ugly only if they cause harm
D) They are ugly because reason deems them so

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the objection that God's creation of non-beneficial things is ugly, showing that ugliness is not intrinsic and that benefit is not incumbent upon the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged an action as inherently evil without considering its context or outcome? Maturidi teaches that ugliness is not intrinsic. How does this challenge your habit of feeling self-righteous about your own actions? Will you now pause before condemning others, recognizing that God alone knows the full reality?
?
You claim to trust God's wisdom, yet you often question His plans when they bring hardship. This lesson shows that benefit is not incumbent upon God and that harm may have purpose. Are you truly submitting to His decree, or are you still demanding that your life must always feel beneficial? What would change if you fully accepted that even apparent non-benefit is not ugly?
?
Maturidi refutes the idea that ugliness is intrinsic. Think of a sin you despise—does your hatred stem from its true nature or from your own subjective perspective? How can you align your moral judgments with divine wisdom, not just your feelings? What step will you take today to check your assumptions about good and evil?

The Wisdom of Giving to a Disobedient Servant
Maturidi addresses the objection that giving ability to a servant who will disobey is unwise, and refutes the Mu'tazila claim regarding providing power for faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that giving power to a disobedient servant is unwise, and why God's gifts can serve purposes beyond immediate obedience.

Core Idea
Gifts can be wise even when the recipient disobeys.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection-and-answer concerning divine wisdom. The opponent argues that it would be foolish for a master to give resources to a servant who will use them to disobey. Maturidi responds by distinguishing the purpose of the gift: it may serve other benefits, not merely faith. He then refutes the Mu'tazila who claim God gives power specifically for belief, knowing it will lead to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection, a counter-objection, a response, a refutation of the Mu'tazila, and a final reaffirmation.
The passage begins with 'ثم قال' and ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'.
2
Identify the initial objection
Find where Maturidi presents the objection: giving something beneficial to one who will disobey is considered ugly. Also note the counter-objection about giving to someone incapable.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi uses an analogy: one may feed a messenger even if knowing he won't believe, because the feeding is for other benefits. Similarly, God may give power not for belief but for other wise purposes.
The key is that the purpose of the gift determines its wisdom.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazila refutation
Maturidi rejects the Mu'tazila claim that God gives power specifically so the person may believe, while knowing he will disbelieve. That would be impossible and not considered wise.
This shows that Maturidi defends God's wisdom without implying futility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : ذلك الذي قيل فيمن يفعل الحاجة . قيل : والأول أيضا قبح ممن لا يملك التقوية لو طلب منه وتضرع إليه . ثم عارض نفسه بمن يدفع إلى عبده ما يعلم يعصيه به فقال : قد يكون ذلك حكمة نحو من يعلم بخير الرسول أنه لا يؤمن ، يجوز أن يطعمه ونحو ذلك مما يعلم من تأمله جهله بما عارض به نفسه ؛ لأن الذي ذكره لا يجوز أن يعطيه ليؤمن بذلك بعد علمه بأنه لا يفعل ، وإنما يعطيه لمنافع سوى هذا . والمعتزلة تزعم أنه أعطى القوة ليؤمن بها ، وهو يعلم أنه يكفر بها ، فليس ذلك مما قدر فى شيء . ثم المعارضة كانت فيمن يعصيه ، فلا أحد يعد نفسه فى الحكماء إذا علم أن عبده بالذى يعطيه يعصيه ولا يكتسب رضاه بل يعمل بعداوته وشتمه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then he said: That which was said about one who does a need. It was said: And the first also is ugly from one who does not have the ability to strengthen if asked and implored. Then he opposed himself regarding one who gives to his servant what he knows he will disobey him with, and said: That may be wisdom, like one who knows of the goodness of the messenger that he will not believe, may be permissible to feed him, and similar to that which he knows from his reflection, his ignorance of what he opposed himself with; because what he mentioned is not permissible to give to him so that he may believe thereby after knowing that he will not do so, but he gives it for benefits besides this. And the Mu'tazila claim that he gave the power so that he may believe by it, while He knows that he will disbelieve by it. That is not something considered at all. Then the objection was about one who disobeys Him, so no one counts himself among the wise if he knows that his servant, by what he gives him, disobeys him and does not acquire his pleasure, but rather acts with enmity and reviling of him. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the wisdom of providing means to one who will disobey. Maturidi presents an opponent's objection: if giving something beneficial to one who will use it to disobey is considered ugly, then it would also be ugly to give to someone incapable even if they ask. But Maturidi then responds by considering the case of giving to a servant whom the master knows will disobey. He argues that such giving may still be wisdom (hikma), analogous to feeding someone whom one knows will not believe—the feeding is not for the purpose of belief but for other benefits. He then refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God gives power (al-quwwa) specifically so that the person may believe, while knowing they will disbelieve; Maturidi considers this impossible. Finally, he reaffirms that no wise person would give to a servant who will use the gift to disobey and revile him. Thus, the objection is answered by distinguishing the purpose of the gift.

Key Terms
الحاجة (al-haja)
التقوية (al-taqwiya)
الحكمة (al-hikma)
المعتزلة (al-mu'tazila)
القوة (al-quwwa)
الكفر (al-kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God giving power to a disbeliever is futile because the person will not believe.
Correction: Maturidi explains that God's gifts may serve other benefits beyond faith; the purpose is not always the specific act of belief.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila claim that Maturidi refutes in this passage?
A) God gives power for no reason
B) God gives power specifically so that the person may believe, even if He knows they will disbelieve
C) God gives power only to those who will obey
D) God does not give power to disbelievers

Key Takeaway
God's giving of power or provisions is not necessarily for the purpose of causing faith; it may serve other wise benefits even if the recipient disobeys.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing from Allah while you are in a state of disobedience, what do you assume about His wisdom? Do you think He is unaware or that He is giving you a chance for repentance, or do you feel entitled? How does this lesson challenge your assumption? What will you change in your attitude today?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that Allah gives power specifically for faith, but Maturidi refutes that. Have you ever felt that a particular trial or gift was given to you for a specific purpose that you didn't fulfill? How does understanding that Allah's gifts may have multiple purposes affect your gratitude and your response to His blessings? What concrete step will you take to use your abilities for obedience despite past failures?
?
Imagine you are the servant in the analogy: you have been given ability, but you use it to disobey. Do you realize that the gift is still from Allah for other benefits? How should that change your attitude towards repenting and using your abilities for obedience? Are you willing to apologize to Allah and start afresh, or will you continue in ingratitude? Make a decision now.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Children and the Absence of Ugliness
Maturidi explains that children's actions are not described as ugly or kufr, and discusses the criteria for wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why children's actions are not classified as immoral or disbelief, and how actions are evaluated as wise or harmful based on benefit and consequence.

Core Idea
Children's actions lack moral categories; only actions with capacity receive such labels.

Maturidi addresses a key point in Islamic theology: that moral and religious descriptors like 'immorality' or 'disbelief' do not apply to children's actions. This passage establishes the foundational principle that such judgments require capacity and awareness. Additionally, it explains how actions are assessed as wisdom (benefit) or harm, and touches on a dialectical argument about consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Notice how Maturidi states his claim about children and then moves to the criteria for wisdom.
The Arabic has some textual variants noted by the editor, but the core meaning is clear.
2
Identify the key claim about children
The passage explicitly states that no description of immorality (fuhsh) or disbelief (kufr) attaches to actions that come from small children. Ask yourself: why is this important?
This connects to the wider issue of moral responsibility and the absence of taklif for children.
3
Understand the criteria for wisdom and harm
Maturidi explains that an action is considered wisdom from the aspect of benefit, but harm from the aspect of harm in the consequence, ingratitude, or opposing the Lord. Reflect on how this relates to the classification of actions.
The dialectical part about the opponent is supplementary; focus on the main principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذا أولى لما يجئ مثل هذا من الصغار لا يلحقه وصف فحش ولا كفر، ولا يجئ منهم فعل غير نافع نفسه يوصف بالحكمة، والذي قال من إصرار النفع فهو من ذلك الوجه حكمة، ولكن من وجه الضرر صار كذلك، وهو ضرر العاقبة أو كفران النعمة أو مخالفة الرب في الفعل. وإذا كان به دفع ولزمه السؤال ولم يلزم خصمه فيما عارضه بالزمن الذي أجابه مما ينقض عليه ليعلم به بعده عن الحق فيها يوافق عليه ويخالف فيه جميعا.
And this is more appropriate, because such [actions] come from small children: no description of immorality or disbelief attaches to them, and there does not come from them an act that is not beneficial in itself that is described as wisdom. And that which is said about insistence on benefit, it is from that aspect wisdom, but from the aspect of harm it becomes so, and that is harm of consequence, or ingratitude for blessing, or opposing the Lord in action. And if it is repelled by him, the question becomes necessary for him, but not for his opponent in what he objected to him in the time[s] he answered, that which invalidates him, so that he may know by it his distance from the truth in that which he agrees upon and disagrees upon altogether.
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory definition of how actions are assessed. Maturidi states that children's actions do not receive the moral descriptors 'immorality' or 'disbelief' because they lack the capacity for such evaluation. He then discusses the attribution of 'wisdom' (hikmah) to an action: it is considered good if it brings benefit, and harmful if it leads to future harm, ingratitude, or disobedience. The passage also addresses a dialectical point: when an opponent raises an objection based on comparing the witnessed (al-shahid) to the unseen (al-gha'ib), Maturidi notes that the objection does not hold consistently because the opponent himself agrees and disagrees selectively, demonstrating his distance from truth.

Key Terms
الصغار
الفحش
الكفر
الحكمة
الضرر
العاقبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That children are considered sinful or that their actions can be described as immoral or disbelief.
Correction: The passage clearly states that such descriptions do not attach to children's actions because they lack the capacity for moral and religious assessment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are children's actions not described as immoral or disbelief?
A) Because children are always obedient.
B) Because children lack the capacity for such descriptions.
C) Because children's actions are always beneficial.
D) Because children are not responsible for their actions.

Key Takeaway
Children's actions are not classified as immoral or disbelief, and actions are evaluated as wise if beneficial, harmful if they lead to harm or ingratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged a child's action as morally wrong, forgetting that they lack the capacity for such labels? How must you change the way you react to children's mistakes after reading this lesson?
?
This lesson warns that insistence on benefit can become harmful if it leads to ingratitude or disobedience to Allah. In what area of your life are you prioritizing short-term gain over obeying Allah, and what harm of consequence are you risking?
?
The dialectical argument exposes inconsistency in the opponent. Where in your own life do you apply Islamic principles selectively—agreeing in some cases and disagreeing in others—thus distancing yourself from the truth? Commit to one specific change.
